[FOLKLORE AND THE PUBLIC DOMAIN]{.stl_10}

[204. ]{.stl_12}[Like in Kenya, an important area of Rwandese
]{.stl_10}[Law on the Protection of Intellectual  Property ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[concerns the treatment of folklore and the
public domain. This legislation  considers folklore and the public
domain as part of the national culture and heritage.  Therefore, in this
jurisdiction, the use, for profit, of work in the public domain,  or
derived  from Rwandan national folklore, shall be made in return for
payment of royalties according  to the conditions determined by the
empowered authority.]{.stl_10}^[179]{.stl_16}^

[This scheme applies to any use of folklore and public domain works,
including video games; therefore, a producer who wishes to incorporate
any of these works (e.g.,  a traditional song or story) into a video
game, shall pay a royalty to the relevant authority according to the
conditions determined by that entity. Finally, that authority shall
allocate a part of the funds collected (25 per cent) to the promotion of
creativity activity.]{.stl_10}

[205. ]{.stl_12}[In conclusion, Rwanda has legislation that protects
authors and the creation of original  works (the Rwandese ]{.stl_10}[Law
on the Protection of Intellectual Property)]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[, but this law does not  establish a
specific regime for video games, as this industry does not yet exist in
Rwanda.  Therefore, the protection of video games in this jurisdiction
shall follow the general rules  stated in the above-mentioned
law.]{.stl_10}

[SENEGAL]{.stl_11}

[206. ]{.stl_12}[Law No. 2008-09 of January 25, 2008, on
]{.stl_10}[Copyright and Neighboring Rights ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[of Senegal  (hereinafter, the Senegalese
]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[)
establishes, in Article 1, that ―]{.stl_15}[the author  of a  work of
the mind shall enjoy in that work an exclusive incorporeal property
right that shall  be enforceable against all persons.]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[‖ Article 12 affirms that ―]{.stl_15}[the
author of a work shall be  the natural person who created it,]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[‖]{.stl_15}^[180\ ]{.stl_16}^[which author
shall have both moral and economic  rights. The Senegalese
]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act ]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[fulfills
the principle of formality-free protection ]{.stl_10}[ reflected in the
Berne Convention when establishing in Article 2 that
―]{.stl_15}[copyright shall arise  from the mere fact of creation; a
work shall be deemed to have been created, irrespective  of any public
disclosure or physical fixation, by the me]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[re fact of realization of the
author's  concept, even if incomplete.‖]{.stl_19
style="font-style:italic;"}

[Another aspect recognized by Senegalese ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act
]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[is the irrelevance of form of
expression, merit or purpose, as Article 5 determines that
]{.stl_10}[―the provisions of the present ]{.stl_19
style="font-style:italic;"}[Law shall protect the rights of authors in
all works of the mind, whatever their form of ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[expression, merit or purpose.‖]{.stl_19
style="font-style:italic;"}

[In this regard, and like in the other jurisdictions, there is no
specific mention of video games in the list of protected works of the
mind contained in Article 6, although the general description would
suffice to provide protection under this law:]{.stl_10}

[―]{.stl_15}[Intellectual creations of a literary or artistic nature
shall be considered works of]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}

[the mind within the meaning of the present Law (...)‖]{.stl_19
style="font-style:italic;"}

[The law establishes a non-exhaustive list of creations that are
protected, such as: works using language, whether literary, scientific
or technical, including computer programs,  and whether written or oral;
dramatic works and other works intended for stage presentation and
productions, choreographic works, circus acts and feats and dumb-show
works; musical works with or without words; works consisting of
consequences of moving]{.stl_10}

[179 ]{.stl_07}[Articles 201 and 202 of the Rwandese ]{.stl_09}[Law on
the Protection of Intellectual Property]{.stl_17
style="font-style:italic;"}[. ]{.stl_09}[180 ]{.stl_07}[The Senegalese
]{.stl_09}[Copyright Act]{.stl_17
style="font-style:italic;"}[.]{.stl_09}

[page 69]{.stl_10}

[images, with or without sound, known as audiovisual works; works of the
visual arts (drawings, paintings, sculpture, architecture, photographic
works) and geographical maps and plans, sketches and three-dimensional
works relating to geography, topography, architecture and
science.]{.stl_10}

[CLASSIFICATION]{.stl_10}

[207. ]{.stl_12}[As noted above, the Senegalese ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act
]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[offers no classification for video
games.  Additionally, case law is silent on the issue simply because the
courts have not been  faced with disputes involving video games. This
can also be explained by the fact that the  video game market in Senegal
remains in an embryonic stage, so the question of the  classification of
video games has not yet arisen.]{.stl_10}

[208. ]{.stl_12}[According to the Senegalese ]{.stl_10}[Copyright
Act]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[, video games may receive
multiple  classifications. Depending on the number of persons
contributing to the creative process,  the games can be defined as
collective or individual works according to the legal nature of  the
relationship between the different parties involved (e.g., cooperation
contract,  employment contract or service agreement). Where more than
one person is involved in  the creation process of a given video game, a
work of joint authorship would
exist,]{.stl_10}^[181\ ]{.stl_16}^[and  the question of different
contributors and their respective rights arises.]{.stl_10}

[CONTRIBUTORS AND TRANSFER OF RIGHTS]{.stl_10}

[209. ]{.stl_12}[The Senegalese ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[recognizes certain moral and economic rights
of authors.  Indeed, Article 12 determines, as explained above, that the
author of a work shall be the  natural person who created it, while
Articles 17 and 18 explain the special situation of the  works created
by an employee. Concerning initial ownership, the existence of
an  employment agreement shall in no way derogate from the enjoyment of
copyright;]{.stl_10}^[182\ ]{.stl_16}^[ nevertheless, there is a
presumption of assignment to the employer.]{.stl_10}^[183]{.stl_16}^

[210. ]{.stl_12}[The economic rights in a work created by an employee in
the context of his or her  employment shall be presumed to be assigned
to the employer under the employment ]{.stl_10}[ agreement, insofar as
the employer's usual activities at the time of creation give grounds
]{.stl_15}[ therefor. An employer that exploits the rights thus assigned
shall pay remuneration  separate from salary to the employee. In the
absence of an agreement between the  parties, the amount of such
remuneration shall be determined by the competent court.]{.stl_10}

[211. ]{.stl_12}[With regard to the transfer of rights, different
situations can exist:]{.stl_10}

[Independent authors]{.stl_10}

[Article 65 of the Senegalese ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[determines that an assignment of rights may
be granted free of charge or for consideration. Where assignment is
granted for consideration, it shall include for the author a
proportional share of the revenue from exploitation of the
work.]{.stl_10}^[184\ ]{.stl_16}^[However, the author's remuneration may
be calculated as a ]{.stl_15}[lump sum]{.stl_10}[ ]{.stl_32}[in the
following cases: where a basis for calculating the proportional share
cannot be practically determined, where the control cost would be
disproportionate to the expected results, and where the use of the work
is only of an accessory nature in relation to the object
exploited.]{.stl_10}^[185.]{.stl_16}^

[181 ]{.stl_07}[Article 23 of the Senegalese ]{.stl_09}[Copyright
Act]{.stl_17 style="font-style:italic;"}[. ]{.stl_09}[182
]{.stl_07}[Article 17 of the Senegalese ]{.stl_09}[Copyright
Act]{.stl_17 style="font-style:italic;"}[. ]{.stl_09}[183
]{.stl_07}[Article 18 of the Senegalese ]{.stl_09}[Copyright
Act]{.stl_17 style="font-style:italic;"}[. ]{.stl_09}[184
]{.stl_07}[Article 65.2 of the Senegalese ]{.stl_09}[Copyright
Act]{.stl_17 style="font-style:italic;"}[. ]{.stl_09}[185
]{.stl_07}[Article 65.3 of the Senegalese ]{.stl_09}[Copyright
Act]{.stl_17 style="font-style:italic;"}[.]{.stl_09}

[page 70]{.stl_10}

[Audiovisual Works]{.stl_10}

[Some experts consider that video games could be protected as
cinematographic works, given the prevalence of this kind of audiovisual
work and its similarities to film. In this regard, Article 81 of the
Senegalese ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[offers a definition of
]{.stl_10}[Audiovisual Production Contracts]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[, as those ―]{.stl_15}[under which several
natural persons undertake, in return for payment, to create an
audiovisual work for a natural or legal person called the producer, who
or which takes the initiative and has the responsibility for creating
the work.]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[‖ There is also a
specific mention of the pr]{.stl_15}[oducers right in Article 82, which
establishes a presumption of assignment according to which a contract
binding the producer and the authors of an audiovisual work, other than
the author of a musical composition with or without words, shall, unless
otherwise stipulated, imply the assignment to the producer of exclusive
exploitation rights in the audiovisual works.]{.stl_10}^[186]{.stl_16}^

[Joint authorship]{.stl_10}

[The Senegalese ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[affirms, at the Explanatory
Statement,]{.stl_10}^[187\ ]{.stl_16}^[an important aspect of the spirit
of this law. Indeed, in contrast to the philosophy underlying Anglo-
American copyright, the interested parties are placed at the center of
the legislation through a clear statement that they are the source of
the intangible wealth which society will then enjoy. Hence, the choice
to enshrine the rights of employed authors and officials; to relinquish
the category of collective works, which, by granting rights to a legal
entity, is incompatible with the personalized approach; to confirm the
existence of strong and perpetual moral rights; to provide a broad and
synthesized definition of the economic prerogatives granted to the
various right owners (eliminating any doubt as to whether such a
definition includes digital exploitation); and to elaborate a contract
law that can compensate for the financially disadvantaged position of
authors and performers in comparison with those who exploit their
work.]{.stl_10}

[The legal framework of works of joint authorship is contained in
Articles 23 to 26 of the Senegalese ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[, holding that ]{.stl_10}[a work that is the
product of collaboration between two or more authors shall be deemed a
work of joint authorship, irrespective of whether it constitutes an
indivisible whole or consists of autonomously created parts]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[. The economic rights and moral rights in
such works shall be owned jointly by all the authors, and the authors
shall therefore exercise those rights by common accord and, in the event
of failure to agree, the courts shall decide. Another important aspect
is that each of the joint authors shall be free to prosecute, on his or
her own behalf, and without the intervention of the others, any
infringement to his or her economic or moral
rights.]{.stl_10}^[188]{.stl_16}^

[FOLKLORE AND THE PUBLIC DOMAIN]{.stl_10}

[212. ]{.stl_12}[Lastly, it has been deemed necessary, for the sake of
consistency, to devote a separate  part of the Senegalese
]{.stl_10}[Copyright Law ]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[--
]{.stl_15}[part four ]{.stl_10}[-- ]{.stl_15}[to the protection of
folklore and the ]{.stl_10}[ ―]{.stl_15}[domaine public payant‖]{.stl_19
style="font-style:italic;"}[, which, from a legal point of view, are
issues on the margins  of  copyright but which to date have been
considered sufficiently closely connected to the  subject of copyright
to be dealt with in the same legal framework.]{.stl_10}

[Currently, many video game producers that are unwilling to obtain
licenses to exploit protected works of authorship decide to use folklore
and public domain works in order to develop a given video game. The
Senegalese legislation considers folklore and public domain to be part
of the national cultural heritage. The specific definition
considers]{.stl_10}

[186 ]{.stl_07}[Article 82 of the Senegalese ]{.stl_09}[Copyright
Act]{.stl_17 style="font-style:italic;"}[.]{.stl_09}

[187 ]{.stl_07}[Section 3, Explanatory Statement of the Senegalese
]{.stl_09}[Copyright Act]{.stl_17 style="font-style:italic;"}[.
]{.stl_09}[188 ]{.stl_07}[Article 24.4 of the Senegalese
]{.stl_09}[Copyright Act]{.stl_17
style="font-style:italic;"}[.]{.stl_09}

[page 71]{.stl_10}

[folklore all literary and artistic productions created by authors
deemed to be of Senegalese nationality that are passed from generation
to generation and constitute one of the basic elements of the
traditional cultural heritage of Senegal.]{.stl_10}^[189]{.stl_16}^

[Therefore, in this jurisdiction, the use, for profit, of works in
the  public domain or derived from Senegalese national folklore, shall
be made in return for payment of royalties according to the conditions
determined by the relevant authority.]{.stl_10}^[190\ ]{.stl_16}^[The
amount of the royalty is determined by the Minister for Culture, and it
shall not exceed 50 per cent of the rate usually paid to authors in
accordance with current contracts or practices.]{.stl_10}

[This scheme applies to any use of folklore and public domain works,
including video games. Therefore, a producer who wishes to incorporate
any of these works (e.g., a traditional song or story) in a video game
shall pay a royalty to the \[relevant\] \[empowered\] authority,
according to the conditions determined by that entity. Finally, that
authority shall allocate a part of the funds collected (25 per cent) for
social and cultural purposes.]{.stl_10}

[213. ]{.stl_12}[In conclusion, although Senegal has legislation that
protects authors and the creation of  original works, Law No. 2008-09 of
January 25, 2008, on ]{.stl_10}[Copyright and
Neighboring  Rights]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[, does not
establish a specific regime for video games, as this industry is
almost  non-existent in Senegal. Therefore, the protection of video
games in this jurisdiction shall  follow the general rules stated in the
above-mentioned law.]{.stl_10}

[SINGAPORE]{.stl_11}

[214. ]{.stl_12}[The ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[(hereinafter, the Singaporean
]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[) (Chapter
63)]{.stl_10}^[191\ ]{.stl_16}^[does not  establish any specific
definition for video games, but the ]{.stl_10}[Copyright (Excluded
Works)  Order ]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[of 2008, in force
until December 31, 2012, which deals with works excluded  from  the
Singaporean ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[, determines the works to which Section
261C(1)(a)]{.stl_10}^[192\ ]{.stl_16}^[of  the Singaporean
]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[(regarding the circumvention of
technological measures)  shall not apply. At its point 4(b), this Order
excludes the application of Section 261C(1) to
]{.stl_10}[ ―]{.stl_15}[any computer program or video game- i. which is
distributed in an obsolete format and  ii.to which access may be gained
only by means of the original medium or hardware in or  with which it
was designed to be used or operated.]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[‖ Such exclusion of protection seems to
]{.stl_15}[ refer only to the relationship between video games and
technological protection measures  and, in our opinion, it would
literally mean that, except for such cases, video games  should enjoy
the same protection afforded to computer programs.]{.stl_10}

[CLASSIFICATION OF VIDEO GAMES]{.stl_10}

[215. ]{.stl_12}[According to the Singapore ]{.stl_10}[Copyright
Act,]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}^[193\ ]{.stl_16}^[any
―]{.stl_15}[literary, dramatic, musical or artistic‖ ]{.stl_19
style="font-style:italic;"}[ work under this law deserves copyright
protection. Additionally, the Singaporean ]{.stl_10}[ Copyright Act
]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[also differentiates ―artistic
works‖, which are paintings, sculptures,  drawings,
]{.stl_15}[ engravings or photographs, whether the work is of artistic
quality or not; and a building or]{.stl_10}

[189 ]{.stl_07}[Article 156 of the Senegalese ]{.stl_09}[Copyright
Act]{.stl_17 style="font-style:italic;"}[.]{.stl_09}

[190 ]{.stl_07}[Articles 157 and 158 of the Senegalese

[191 ]{.stl_07}[Copyright Act]{.stl_17 style="font-style:italic;"}[,
Chapter 63, approved on 1987 and current version from 2006.]{.stl_09}

[192]{.stl_25}[   ]{.stl_26}[Circumvention of technological measures
261C.]{.stl_09}[---]{.stl_08}[(1) Subject to sections 261D and 261E,
where a]{.stl_17 style="font-style:italic;"}

[technological measure is applied to a copy of a work or other
subject-matter by or with the authorization  of the owner of the

[page 72]{.stl_10}

[model of a building, whether the building or model is of artistic
quality or not. Finally, the law also offers protection to computer
programs, which means:]{.stl_10}

[―]{.stl_15}[an expression, in any language, code or notation, of a set
of instructions (whether with or without related information) intended,
either directly or after either or both of the following: (a) conversion
to another language, code or notation; (b) reproduction in a different
material form, to cause a device having information processing
capabilities to perform a ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[particular function.‖]{.stl_19
style="font-style:italic;"}^[194]{.stl_16}^

[All of these types of works (e.g., scripts, models of buildings,
paintings, characters, and computer code) can be found in video games,
and all of them, individually, are protected under the Singaporean
]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[.]{.stl_10}

[Nonetheless, video games, as a whole, are mainly computer programs
according to the Singapore legislation. When developing software, the
starting point is usually to identify the problem or task that the
program is to solve or perform. In doing this, the programmer prepares
written documentation ]{.stl_10}[-- ]{.stl_15}[notes, flow charts and
algorithms ]{.stl_10}[-- ]{.stl_15}[which constitutes the source
documents of the final work. These source documents enjoy copyright
protection, according to the Singaporean ]{.stl_10}[Copyright
Act]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[, as a type of artistic or
literary work, where they fulfill the requirements of the law.]{.stl_10}

[216. ]{.stl_12}[Once these source documents are prepared, the
programmer will then write a program in  a high-level language (i.e.,
Basic, Cobol, Fortran or Java);]{.stl_10}^[195\ ]{.stl_16}^[computer
programs are ]{.stl_10}[ considered ―]{.stl_15}[literal
expressions]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[‖, and in the copyright
]{.stl_15}[system will therefore fall under  Section 7A of the
Singaporean ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[, which includes computer programs in the
]{.stl_10}[ definition of ―]{.stl_15}[literary works‖]{.stl_19
style="font-style:italic;"}[. ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[According to ]{.stl_10}[Halsbury's Laws of
Singapore]{.stl_19 style="font-style:italic;"}[, a computer  program is
eligible for copyright and is protected as a literary work. Both the
source and  object codes are entitled to copyright
protection.]{.stl_10}^[196\ ]{.stl_16}^

[217. ]{.stl_12}[Computer programs are therefore accorded copyright
protection where the form of ]{.stl_10}[ expression is original, which
is the author's original (intellectual) creation]{.stl_15}[.
Nonetheless, ]{.stl_10}[ video games can also be classified as
―audiovisual works‖ where they comprise both ]{.stl_15}[ audio
recordings and artistic renditions. ]{.stl_10}[Accordingly, audiovisual
works ―]{.stl_15}[means a sound  recording, a cinematograph film, a
sound broadcast, a television broadcast or a cable ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[ programme‖,]{.stl_19
style="font-style:italic;"}^[197\ ]{.stl_06
style="font-style:italic;"}^[and cinematograph films means:]{.stl_10}

[―]{.stl_15}[the aggregate of visual images embodied in an article or
thing so as to be capable by the use of that article or thing (a) of
being shown as a moving picture; or (b) of being embodied in another
article or thing by the use of which it can be so shown, and includes
the aggregate of the sounds embodied in a sound-track associated with
such visual images]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[.‖]{.stl_15}

[218. ]{.stl_12}[Therefore, in accordance with the Singaporean
legislation, one needs to analyze the  specific elements of a given
video game in order to ascertain its legal classification, as this  will
depend on the computer program and the audiovisual (and
cinematographic)  components included in the game.]{.stl_10}

[RIGHTS HOLDERS AND STAKEHOLDERS]{.stl_10}

[219. ]{.stl_12}[The main stakeholders include the computer programmers,
the producer/investor funding  the development of the video game, the
artistic and creative talent involved in the project  and the
publisher.]{.stl_10}

[The Singaporean ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[provides a narrow definition of an author.
Throughout the text, it determines that copyright shall subsist in an
original literary, dramatic, musical or artistic work that is
unpublished and of which the author was a qualified person at the time
when the work was made; or, where the making of the work extended over a
period of time, the author was a qualified person for a substantial part
of that period.]{.stl_10}^[198]{.stl_16}^

[220. ]{.stl_12}[With respect to video games, the author will be the
person/s who originated the  expression of the work, including all its
elements (e.g., computer software, graphics and  sounds). However, most
video games currently being developed are created by more  than one
person, or commissioned by one studio. In many cases, these are works of
joint ]{.stl_10}[ authorship, which means ―]{.stl_15}[a work that has
been produced by the collaboration of two or  more authors and in which
the contribution of each author is not separate from the ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[ contribution of the other author or the
contributions of other authors.‖]{.stl_19 style="font-style:italic;"}

[221. ]{.stl_12}[Another question that has arisen due to the development
of modern technologies is the  issue of the works created by gamers
involved in online gaming. Users may have been  given rights to modify
the underlying code or to create a new work from the original work,  in
which case the new creation is a derivative work and the new program
content may be  exclusively owned by the user/player, which excludes the
original content or work. The  new work must be original, recorded and a
product of effort. However, the Singapore  courts have not yet come
across cases involving the legal rights and limitations
of  player-  characters/user-creators. ]{.stl_10}[Players' rights are
generally set out in the End]{.stl_15}[-User License  Agreement between
the game provider and the gamer. Additionally, it is common to see  a
term that provides for game providers to own everything in the virtual
world.]{.stl_10}

[RIGHTS REGIME]{.stl_10}

[222. ]{.stl_12}[Concerning the ownership of copyright in original works
where they have been created  under an employment
relationship,]{.stl_10}^[199\ ]{.stl_16}^[there is no highly developed
regime in Singapore.  Thus, generally, where a literary, dramatic or
artistic work (including computer code and  musical works) is made by
the author in pursuance of the terms of his or her employment  by
another person under a contract of service or apprenticeship, that other
person shall be  entitled to any copyright subsisting in the work by
virtue of this part.]{.stl_10}

[223. ]{.stl_12}[In any other case, copyright shall be transmissible by
assignment; however, no  assignment of copyright (whether total or
partial) shall have effect unless it is in writing  and signed by or on
behalf of the assignor. Therefore, where a Singaporean video
game  studio hires an independent contractor to develop certain elements
of a video game, there  is no a presumption that rights are assigned to
the producer, which, therefore, shall act  diligently and include in the
service agreement the assignment of the copyrights in
its  favor.]{.stl_10}

[ECONOMIC COMPENSATION]{.stl_10}

[198 ]{.stl_07}[Section 27 of the Singaporean ]{.stl_09}[Copyright
Act]{.stl_17 style="font-style:italic;"}[:
]{.stl_09}[―]{.stl_08}[Original works in which copyright
subsists.]{.stl_09}[‖ ]{.stl_08}[199 ]{.stl_07}[Section 30 of the
Singaporean ]{.stl_09}[Copyright Act]{.stl_17
style="font-style:italic;"}[.]{.stl_09}

[page 74]{.stl_10}

[224. ]{.stl_12}[For an employee, compensation for a creative
contribution may be included in the salary,  as the work is done in the
course of employment, or may be alternatively structured
as  contractually agreed upon between the employer and the employee.
Singapore law is  silent on the case of independent contractors and,
therefore, compensation, a purely  commercial decision, would be subject
to an agreement between the parties.]{.stl_10}

[SOUTH AFRICA]{.stl_11}

[225. ]{.stl_12}[The main source of copyright law in South Africa is the
]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act ]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[as
amended  (hereinafter, the South African ]{.stl_10}[Copyright
Act]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[), which regulates all aspects
of copyright  in  the country, including the rights to compensation,
acknowledgement, authorship, usage  and distribution of
works.]{.stl_10}^[200\ ]{.stl_16}^[This legislation follows broadly the
United Kingdom and  Commonwealth copyright traditions. Furthermore,
South Africa is party to the ]{.stl_10}[Berne  Convention for the
Protection of Literary and Artistic Works]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}^[201\ ]{.stl_16}^[as well as to the
]{.stl_10}[Agreement  on Trade-Related Aspects of Intellectual Property
Rights]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}^[202\ ]{.stl_16}^[(TRIPS
Agreement).]{.stl_10}

[CLASSIFICATION OF VIDEO GAMES]{.stl_10}

[226. ]{.stl_12}[There is no specific mention of video games in South
African ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[,
more  precisely in Article 2 regarding works eligible for copyright.
Nonetheless, video games are ]{.stl_10}[ legally classified as
―cinematograph films‖ in South Africa,]{.stl_15}^[203\ ]{.stl_16}^[as
was decided in the  Supreme Court of Appeals case of ]{.stl_10}[Golden
China TV Game Centre and Others v.  Nintendo Co Ltd.]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}^[204]{.stl_16}^

[A ―cinematograph film‖ is defined by the South African
]{.stl_15}[Copyright Act]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}^[205\ ]{.stl_16}^[as:]{.stl_10}

[―]{.stl_15}[any fixation or storage by any means whatsoever on film or
any other material of data, signals or a sequence of images capable,
when used in conjunction with any other mechanical, electronic or other
device, of being seen as a moving picture and of reproduction, and
includes the sounds embodied in a sound-track associated with the film,
]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[but shall not include a computer
program.‖]{.stl_19 style="font-style:italic;"}^[206]{.stl_16}^

[Nonetheless, given the complexity of video games, other elements can
also be copyrighted, including the computer program used to run the
work. Such software is also protected under the South African
]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[, which
includes (i) a version of the program in a programming language, code or
notation different from that of the program; or (ii) a fixation of the
program in or on a medium different from the medium of fixation of the
program.]{.stl_10}

[AUTHORS]{.stl_10}

[200 ]{.stl_07}[Act 98 of 1978.]{.stl_09}

[201 ]{.stl_07}[Berne Convention for the Protection of Literary and
Artistic Works ]{.stl_17 style="font-style:italic;"}[of September 9,
1886, completed at Paris]{.stl_09}

[on May 4, 1896.]{.stl_09}

^[202]{.stl_30}^[. The TRIPS Agreement is Annex 1C of the
]{.stl_09}[Marrakesh Agreement Establishing the World Trade
Organization]{.stl_17 style="font-style:italic;"}[,]{.stl_09}

[signed in Marrakesh, Morocco, on April 15, 1994.]{.stl_09}

^[203]{.stl_30}^[. ]{.stl_09}[―]{.stl_08}[New technologies and old
copyrights: the protection of video games as cinematograph
films]{.stl_09}[‖  ]{.stl_08}[(1995) 7 ]{.stl_09}[South African
Mercantile Law Journal ]{.stl_17
style="font-style:italic;"}[112]{.stl_09}[--]{.stl_08}[120.]{.stl_09}

^[204]{.stl_30}^[. 1997 (1) SA 405 (SCA).]{.stl_09}

^[205]{.stl_30}^[. The South African ]{.stl_09}[Copyright Act]{.stl_17
style="font-style:italic;"}[.]{.stl_09}

[206 ]{.stl_07}[Article 1 of the South African ]{.stl_09}[Copyright Act
]{.stl_17 style="font-style:italic;"}[at
]{.stl_09}[―]{.stl_08}[Definitions]{.stl_09}[‖]{.stl_08}[.]{.stl_09}

[page 75]{.stl_10}

[227. ]{.stl_12}[According to the South African ]{.stl_10}[Copyright
Act]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[, the ―]{.stl_15}[author of a
cinematograph film is the  person by whom the arrangements ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[for the making of the film were
made.‖]{.stl_19
style="font-style:italic;"}^[207\ ]{.stl_16}^[Generally,  video game
studios arrange all the elements necessary (including financial) to
produce  video games; in such a case, the studio shall be the author in
accordance with the Act.]{.stl_10}

[In order for an author or authors to copyright their work, he or she
needs to be what is ]{.stl_10}[defined as a ―qualified person‖, which
is, in the case of an individual, a person who is a ]{.stl_15}[South
African citizen or who is domiciled or resident in the country; or, in
the case of a legal person, a body incorporated under the laws of the
country.]{.stl_10}^[208\ ]{.stl_16}^[Regardless, works
copyright-protected in other Berne Convention countries are
copyright-protected in South Africa and ]{.stl_10}[vice versa]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[.]{.stl_10}

[228. ]{.stl_12}[Where an employer-employee relationship exists, the
South African ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[ provides that, as a general rule, the
ownership of the copyright vests in the author, except  where a person
commissions the making of a cinematograph film and pays for it
pursuant  to the commission, in which case the owner of the copyright
subsisting in the work so  made will be the commissioner (in other
words, the employer).]{.stl_10}^[209\ ]{.stl_16}^[In this context,
the  employee would be considered the author, while the employer is
considered the owner of  the copyrights. The same rule applies for works
made in the course of an employment  agreement, in which case the
employer will be the copyright owner, while the employee  will be
considered the author.]{.stl_10}

[STAKEHOLDERS AND TRANSFER OF RIGHTS]{.stl_10}

[229. ]{.stl_12}[Only a person who is the owner of the copyright in the
cinematograph film may submit a  copyright application to the
Registration of Copyright in Cinematograph Films,  and only  owners and
their successors in title have the right to authorize or to prohibit
the  commercial sale or rental to the public of originals or copies of
their copyrighted works. In  South Africa, the owner of the copyrighted
video game would depend on the contractual  nexus between the developing
parties and their local subsidiaries, including whether such  contracts
allow for a cession of ownership and/or enforcement rights.]{.stl_10}

[South African copyrights are governed by a legislative regime. As
mentioned above, the South African ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[, the ]{.stl_10}[Berne Convention]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[, the ]{.stl_10}[TRIPS Agreement ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[and other international treaties to which
South Africa is a member provide the framework for the administration
and regulation of copyrights.]{.stl_10}

[230. ]{.stl_12}[However, there are contractual, automatic and
presumptive regimes that govern the way  owners, distributors, retailers
and end-users use copyrighted works. In order to distribute  and market
the works copyrighted by and belonging to the owner, the parties along
the  chain of supply will set up agreements that entail copyright
transfer to the other parties, in  part or completely. This may be in
the form of a:]{.stl_10}

[a. ]{.stl_10}[―Non]{.stl_15}[-]{.stl_10}[exclusive‖ license: this can
be in a written or verbal format]{.stl_15}[. It can be]{.stl_10}

[revoked, and more than one license is
possible.]{.stl_10}^[210]{.stl_16}^

[b. ]{.stl_10}[―Exclusive‖ license: this must be in writing and for a
prescribed term]{.stl_15}[. It]{.stl_10}

[precludes use by anyone else without written
consent.]{.stl_10}^[211]{.stl_16}^

[207 ]{.stl_07}[Ibid]{.stl_17 style="font-style:italic;"}[.]{.stl_09}

[208 ]{.stl_07}[Section 3(1) of the South African ]{.stl_09}[Copyright
Act]{.stl_17 style="font-style:italic;"}[. ]{.stl_09}[209
]{.stl_07}[Section 21(1)(c) of the South African ]{.stl_09}[Copyright
Act]{.stl_17 style="font-style:italic;"}[. ]{.stl_09}[210
]{.stl_07}[Section 22 of the South African ]{.stl_09}[Copyright
Act]{.stl_17 style="font-style:italic;"}[. ]{.stl_09}[211
]{.stl_07}[Ibid]{.stl_17 style="font-style:italic;"}[.]{.stl_09}

[page 76]{.stl_10}

[231. ]{.stl_12}[Despite these contractual arrangements, the author
always retains the moral rights, which  cannot be transferred. These
give the author the right to be named as the author of the  work. Moral
rights are aime]{.stl_10}[d at protecting the author's work from
distortion, mutilation or  other circumstances that could harm the
author's reputation.]{.stl_15}^[212]{.stl_16}^

[232. ]{.stl_12}[Copyrights are furthermore transferred by testamentary
disposition, automatically passing ]{.stl_10}[ to an heir's estate by
inheritance in ]{.stl_15}[the same way as an asset does. When
an  unpublished work is inherited, the copyright, including the
protection of moral rights, is  inherited as well. Transfer occurs by
operation of law if the copyright holder becomes  insolvent in order to
service his or her other debts.]{.stl_10}^[213]{.stl_16}^

[FINANCIAL COMPENSATION AVAILABLE FOR AUTHORS AND OWNERS]{.stl_10}

[233. ]{.stl_12}[The owner (or author, if he or she is also the owner of
the copyrights) is entitled to  equitable remuneration for the use of
the work belonging to him or her by others, for  example through
royalties, which are typically agreed upon between the parties. A
license  agreement defines the terms under which the copyrights are
licensed by one party to  another, and the remuneration will depend on
the type of license and the restrictions on  that license, as agreed
upon by the parties.]{.stl_10}

[234. ]{.stl_12}[In essence, the manner in which the employee is
remunerated will depend entirely on ]{.stl_10}[ what was agreed upon in
an employee's contract of employment, or in the service
]{.stl_15}[ agreement. However, according to the ]{.stl_10}[Basic
Conditions of Employment Act, ]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[an
employer  must pay to an employee any remuneration that is paid in
money.]{.stl_10}^[214\ ]{.stl_16}^[A true independent  contractor will
be paid in a lump sum once performance is complete or in proportion to
the  stage of completeness, as set out in the contract between him or
her and the contractor.  In the latter case, the contractor will invoice
the employer at intervals and will be paid  accordingly. No mandatory
royalties are required by the South African ]{.stl_10}[Copyright
Act]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[.]{.stl_10}

[CONTRIBUTIONS BY PLAYERS INVOLVED IN INTERACTIVE ONLINE
GAMING]{.stl_10}

[235. ]{.stl_12}[This is an area of law that may be problematic in the
South African context. According to  the South African
]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[, the
ownership of any copyright in a work shall vest in the  author or, in
the case of a work of joint authorship, in the ]{.stl_10}[co-authors
]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[of the
work.]{.stl_10}^[215\ ]{.stl_16}^[A ]{.stl_10}[ ―work of joint
authorship‖ means a work produced by the collaboration of two or more
]{.stl_15}[ authors in which the contribution of each author is not
separable from the contribution of  the other author or authors. It
could be argued in these circumstances that the  contributions of an
online gamer could be considered a collaboration or
contribution.  Furthermore, the copyright shall be conferred on every
work eligible for copyright, of  which  the author or, in the case of a
work of joint authorship, any one of the authors is a person  who is a
South African citizen or is domiciled or resident in the
country.]{.stl_10}^[216]{.stl_16}^

[However, most online gaming platforms will be the subject of terms and
conditions for use, which may, ]{.stl_10}[inter alia, ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[take the form of an End-User License
Agreement or  Software License Agreement, enforceable by the South
African courts. An end-user license agreement or software license
agreement is a contract between the licensor  and ]{.stl_10}[purchaser,
establishing the purchaser's right to use the software]{.stl_15}[. It is
this contract that will determine the status of interactive online
players.]{.stl_10}

[212 ]{.stl_07}[Section 20 of the South African ]{.stl_09}[Copyright
Act]{.stl_17 style="font-style:italic;"}[. ]{.stl_09}[213
]{.stl_07}[Section 22 of the South African ]{.stl_09}[Copyright
Act]{.stl_17 style="font-style:italic;"}[. ]{.stl_09}[214 ]{.stl_07}[Act
75 of 1997.]{.stl_09}

[215 ]{.stl_07}[Section 21(1)(a) ]{.stl_09}[(supra ]{.stl_17
style="font-style:italic;"}[note 16) of the South African
]{.stl_09}[Copyright Act]{.stl_17 style="font-style:italic;"}[.
]{.stl_09}[216 ]{.stl_07}[Section 3(1)(a) of the South African
]{.stl_09}[Copyright Act]{.stl_17
style="font-style:italic;"}[.]{.stl_09}

[page 77]{.stl_10}

[For example, the End-User License Agreement for PlayStation
3,]{.stl_10}^[217\ ]{.stl_16}^[one of the most popular online
interactive gaming platforms in South Africa, provides that all titles,
content, publisher names, trademarks, artwork and associated imagery are
the trademarks and/or copyright material of the owners, that being, in
this case, Sony Entertainment Limited. The agreement provides that an
online gamer may not lease, rent, sublicense, publish, modify, adapt,
translate any portion, broadcast, reverse engineer, decompile or
disassemble any portion of the game software, or create any derivative
works therefrom. Furthermore, the gamer may not resell the game
software. The agreement clearly provides that the online gamer does not
have any ownership rights or interests in the system software.]{.stl_10}

[236. ]{.stl_12}[In South Africa, contracts wherein a user does not
sign, but accepts the terms and  conditions by accessing the software,
are enforceable according to Sections 11, 12 and  13 under the
]{.stl_10}[Electronic Communications and Transactions Act.]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}^[218\ ]{.stl_16}^[Section 13 in
particular  provides that, where an electronic signature is not required
by the parties to an electronic  transaction, an expression of intent or
other statement is not without legal force if such ]{.stl_10}[ person's
intent or other statement can be inferred]{.stl_15}[. This provides for
so-]{.stl_10}[called ―click‖ ]{.stl_15}[ agreements, where a user simply
indicates acceptance of terms and conditions as  binding.]{.stl_10}

[Therefore, in order to determine who will own the copyrights in a work
created by a gamer, one must first analyze the end-user license
agreement imposed by the platform owner to determine if there is a
transfer of rights from the gamer/author to the video game studio, and
its validity according to South African law.]{.stl_10}

[237. ]{.stl_12}[As in other jurisdictions, South Africa affirms the
distributive classification of video games,  offering protection through
different means, including software, cinematograph films
and  characters. ]{.stl_10}[Nonetheless, certain issues relating to
authors' and contributors' rights under ]{.stl_15}[ the South African
]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act ]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[remain
unclear, such as where it affirms that the author of  a cinematograph
film is the person who made the arrangements for making the film,
but  afterwards establishes that, in employment relationships, the
employee is the author while  the employer is the owner of the
copyrights. Therefore, gaps still remain that could be  addressed by
future legislation.]{.stl_10}

[SPAIN]{.stl_11}

[CLASSIFICATION OF VIDEO GAMES]{.stl_10}

[238. ]{.stl_12}[Intellectual property is regulated in Spain mainly
through the ]{.stl_10}[Royal Legislative Decree  1/1996 ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[of April 12, 1996, which approves the
]{.stl_10}[Consolidated Text of the Law on
Intellectual  Property]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[,
regularizing, clarifying and harmonizing the applicable statutory
provisions,  which has undergone many amendments in recent years
(hereinafter, the Spanish ]{.stl_10}[ Copyright Act]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[). As is the case in other jurisdictions,
Spanish law protects ]{.stl_10}[―all original ]{.stl_19
style="font-style:italic;"}[ literary, artistic or scientific creations
expressed in any manner or medium, whether ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[ tangible or intangible, that is known at
the present or may be invented in the future.‖]{.stl_19
style="font-style:italic;"}^[219\ ]{.stl_16}^[ The text also provides a
non-exhaustive list of works that can be protected under this law  and,
although video games are not specifically mentioned, musical
compositions,  cinematographic and other audiovisual works, plans,
models, drawings and computer  programs are (among others). Therefore,
there is no reference to video games ]{.stl_10}[per se ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[in]{.stl_10}

[217 ]{.stl_07}[[PlayStation 3 End-User License Agreement, available
online at
http://www.scei.co.jp/ps3-]{.stl_09}](http://www.scei.co.jp/ps3-){target="_blank"}

[eula/ps3_eula_en.html \[last accessed: July 6, 2012\]. ]{.stl_09}[218
]{.stl_07}[Act 25 of 2002.]{.stl_09}

[219 ]{.stl_07}[Article 10 of the Spanish ]{.stl_09}[Copyright
Act]{.stl_17 style="font-style:italic;"}[.]{.stl_09}

[page 78]{.stl_10}

[the Spanish legislation neither does it provide a legal classification
or a defined list of their characteristics.]{.stl_10}

[239. ]{.stl_12}[Nonetheless, since the Spanish ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act
]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[considers that any original
artistic work is  eligible for copyright protection, this kind of modern
work certainly does not lack  protection. The issue then relates to the
specific nature of that protection for video games  and the applicable
legal regime.]{.stl_10}

[In this sense, video games are a new kind of work that, in general
terms, comprises multiple elements, e.g., graphs, maps, photographs,
audiovisual works, musical works, choreographies, characters and
scripts. In this context, the Spanish ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[protects any individual element of a video
game, as long as it is original and creative. The question here concerns
how a video game should be protected as a whole, sole work of
authorship. Some scholars think that video games are, essentially,
]{.stl_10}[multimedia works ]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[(which
are not defined by the Spanish ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[) running on a computer
program,]{.stl_10}^[220\ ]{.stl_16}^[while others hold that these
multimedia works are essentially framed within the category of
audiovisual works, following the majority of international doctrine and
jurisprudence.]{.stl_10}^[221]{.stl_16}^

[240. ]{.stl_12}[According to Article 86 of the Spanish
]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[,
cinematographic and other ]{.stl_10}[ audiovisual works are
―]{.stl_15}[creations expressed by means of a series of associated
images,  with or without incorporated sound, that are intended
essentially to be shown by means of  projection apparatus or any other
means of communication to the public of the images ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[ and of the sound.‖ ]{.stl_19
style="font-style:italic;"}[Some authors, including Francisco Javier
Donaire Villa and Antonio  José Planells de la Maza, have considered
that this definition excludes the qualification of  video games as
audiovisual works, as they do not fulfill one of the basic requirements
of  the article, which is that these works essentially are not intended
to be shown by means of  projection apparatus, but to be played in an
interactive way.]{.stl_10}^[222]{.stl_16}^

[241. ]{.stl_12}[Spanish jurisprudence has not yet addressed the
question of the classification of video  games. Most of the judicial
cases involving video games have been in criminal courts,  regarding
piracy (in terms of both the video games themselves and the
relevant  technological protection measures). When analyzing these
issues, criminal courts (which  are not copyright specialists) have
treated video games as software, applying the specific  regime for this
kind of work of authorship,]{.stl_10}^[223\ ]{.stl_16}^[although this
may be because technological  protection measures are usually aimed at
protecting the game software.]{.stl_10}

[In 2003, the ]{.stl_10}[Audiencia Provincial de Barcelona ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[(Civil Court of
Appeal)]{.stl_10}^[224\ ]{.stl_16}^[heard a case regarding the
infringement of the rights of the developer of the famous video
game  ]{.stl_10}[Tomb Raider]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[, by a
magazine publisher that had issued an article which included a model
portraying the famous video game character, even with erotic
connotations. The ruling said that Article 10 of the Spanish
]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act ]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[includes,
as a matter of example, a ]{.stl_10}[numerus apertus ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[list of protectable works, including
audiovisual works, software or drawings although it did not clarify
whether the mention of these three types of works indicated that the
tribunal considered that video games contained these specific elements
rather than others. In any case, the court ruled against the defendant,
holding that the]{.stl_10}

[220 ]{.stl_07}[―Manual de propiedad intelectual‖, ]{.stl_18
style="font-style:italic;"}[by Rodrigo Bercovitz, p. 279.]{.stl_09}

[221 ]{.stl_07}[―]{.stl_08}[La Protección Jurídica de los Derechos de
Autor de los Creadores de Videojuegos]{.stl_17
style="font-style:italic;"}[‖, by Francisco  Javier]{.stl_08}

[Donaire Villa and Antonio José Planells de la Maza, pp.
113-117.]{.stl_09}

[222 ]{.stl_07}[―]{.stl_08}[La Protección Jurídica de los Derechos de
Autor de los Creadores de Videojuegos]{.stl_17
style="font-style:italic;"}[‖, by Francisco  Javier]{.stl_08}

[Donaire Villa and Antonio José Planells de la Maza, p. 118.]{.stl_09}

[223 ]{.stl_07}[For example, the sentence of the Court of Appeal
Audiencia Provincial de las Islas Baleares  of March 31,]{.stl_09}

[2001, and of the Court of First Instance Juzgado de lo Penal nº 4 de
Barcelona, of January 28, 2004. ]{.stl_09}[224 ]{.stl_07}[Sentence of
the Audiencia Provincial de Barcelona of May 28, 2003.]{.stl_09}

[page 79]{.stl_10}

[publisher infringed the rights (both the moral and the patrimonial) of
the video game studio when the popular video game character was depicted
by the model.]{.stl_10}^[225]{.stl_16}^

[242. ]{.stl_12}[Therefore, and although most of the criminal courts
analyzing cases regarding video  games have considered these to be
mainly computer programs, the question of their legal  classification
remains unanswered. One school of thought holds that video games
should  be protected as a whole, not as an audiovisual work of
individually-protected elements  (e.g., software, images, graphics,
sounds, characters and script).]{.stl_10}

[STAKEHOLDERS, AUTHORS AND CONTRIBUTORS]{.stl_10}

[243. ]{.stl_12}[A video game is a work that requires the involvement
and participation of a great number  of professionals, including, from
the creative or technical side, sound engineers, software  programmers
and animators, and from the business side, producers,
marketing  specialists, publishers and online platform operators. All of
them contribute to the game  although, in principle, only those that
develop a creative and original work are entitled to  receive copyright
protection.]{.stl_10}

[According to the Spanish ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[, Article 5, ]{.stl_10}[―the natural person
who creates any ]{.stl_19 style="font-style:italic;"}[literary, artistic
or scientific work shall be considered the author thereof. Nevertheless,
the protection that this Law confers on the author may be enjoyed by
legal entities under the ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[circumstances expressly provided for
therein.‖ ]{.stl_19 style="font-style:italic;"}[Therefore, legal
entities cannot become ]{.stl_10}[authors]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[, but can acquire almost all of the
exploitation rights (excepting the unwaivable remuneration rights), on
an exclusive basis, and with almost no limitation.]{.stl_10}

[244. ]{.stl_12}[Where a video game is created by more than one author
(which is customary in this  industry), there are two possible rights
regimes to consider: (1) Works of Joint Authorship;  and (2) Collective
Works. Regarding the first category, Article 7 of the Spanish
]{.stl_10}[Copyright  Act ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[establishes that the rights in a work that
is the unitary result of the collaboration of two  or more authors shall
belong to all of them, in proportions determined by them. In  contrast,
a collective work is created at the:]{.stl_10}

[―]{.stl_15}[initiative and under the direction of a person, whether
natural person or legal entity, who edits it and publishes it under his
name, and where it consists of the combination of contributions by
various authors whose personal contributions are so integrated in the
single, autonomous creation for which they have been made that it is not
possible to ascribe to any one of them a separate right in the whole
work so made.]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[‖]{.stl_15}^[226]{.stl_16}^

[In this case, the rights in the collective work shall vest in the
person who publishes it in his or her name, which can be a natural
person or a corporation.]{.stl_10}

[Indeed, corporations usually assume such a role in the video game
industry, that is, they take the initiative and direct a group of
persons and resources to create the video game. The issue here is that,
if one considers that video games are ]{.stl_10}[audiovisual
works]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[, then they cannot be
considered collective works, as the Spanish ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act
]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[imposes (with the aim of
protecting authors) that audiovisual works are ]{.stl_10}[works of joint
authorship ]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[and not collective
works.]{.stl_10}^[227\ ]{.stl_16}^[Therefore, the classification of
video games is also important when analyzing whether they are, according
to Spanish law, collective works (which seems to]{.stl_10}

[225 ]{.stl_07}[This conclusion was quite controversial in Spain, since
Article 5 of the Spanish  ]{.stl_09}[Copyright Act ]{.stl_17
style="font-style:italic;"}[holds that only]{.stl_09}

[natural persons can be authors, so only they can have moral rights, and
since the plaintiff in this case was a]{.stl_09}

[corporation with, in principle, no moral rights.]{.stl_09}

[226 ]{.stl_07}[Article 8 of the Spanish ]{.stl_09}[Copyright
Act]{.stl_17 style="font-style:italic;"}[.]{.stl_09}

[227 ]{.stl_07}[Article 87 of the Spanish ]{.stl_09}[Copyright
Act]{.stl_17 style="font-style:italic;"}[.]{.stl_09}

[page 80]{.stl_10}

[be a suitable category based on the way in which video games are
usually developed) or works of joint authorship (which is more favorable
to authors).]{.stl_10}

[In any event, an author will always be the natural person who has
created a work, pursuant to Article 5 of the Spanish
]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[, while
what will vary is the rights regime in either case.]{.stl_10}

[245. ]{.stl_12}[Regarding individuals/gamers involved in online or
offline gaming, creating new works  for  a given video game by using the
tools provided by the game developer (for example, the  popular game
]{.stl_10}[Little Big Planet]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[, in
which user creation of levels and settings is one of its  essential
elements), the rights regime is described below. In such cases, the new
work  can be considered an independent
work]{.stl_10}^[228\ ]{.stl_16}^[(where it constitutes a new creation)
or a  derivative work (where it transforms a previous artistic work,
which can be a stage of the  game).]{.stl_10}

[246. ]{.stl_12}[In that case, gamers will be entitled to copyright
protection as long as they create an  original work of authorship. The
copyright in such works will vest initially in the creator,  unless
there is a contractual relationship between the gamer and a third
person  (generally, the video game studio or the owner of the online
platform). Gamers involved in  online gaming usually have to accept
certain terms and conditions in order to play in an  online environment
or to share their creations with the gaming community
(so-]{.stl_10}[called ―End]{.stl_15}[- ]{.stl_10}[ User License
Agreements‖)]{.stl_15}[. Therefore, the game studio shall determine who
owns the  copyright or what kind of license is granted when uploading a
certain creation to an online  platform.]{.stl_10}

[WHO OWNS THE RIGHTS]{.stl_10}

[247. ]{.stl_12}[According to the Spanish ]{.stl_10}[Copyright
Act]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[, in principle the author is
the owner of the  copyrights in the work created by him or her; however,
in the event of a work of joint  authorship, the rights belong to all of
the authors (unless they have agreed otherwise),  and where a collective
work exists, the person who took the initiative and coordinated
the  work is the holder of the copyrights.]{.stl_10}

[The Spanish ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[regulates the transfer of rights, holding
that ]{.stl_10}[―the exploitation ]{.stl_19
style="font-style:italic;"}[rights in the work may be transferred by
]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[inter vivos
]{.stl_10}[transaction, the transfer being limited to the right or
rights transferred, to the means of exploitation expressly provided for
and ]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[the time and territorial scope
specified.‖]{.stl_19
style="font-style:italic;"}^[229\ ]{.stl_16}^[Therefore, any transfer of
rights must be evidenced in writing, and if the parties fail to
establish the scope of the transfer, Article 43 deems that the duration
of the transfer shall be five years, the territorial scope is the
country in which the transfer is effected, and the exploitation rights
those necessarily deduced from the contract itself and that are
essential to fulfilling the purpose of  the contract.]{.stl_10}

[248. ]{.stl_12}[As a consequence, where a video game studio hires a
freelancer to develop certain  elements of a game, the company must
acquire the exploitation rights of the original  author of the content
they will include in the video game by signing a license agreement or  a
service agreement stipulating the transfer of rights therein. In such
documents, the  video game studio must ensure that the scope of the
transfer is properly reflected,  including the exclusivity of the
granting, the unlimited territorial and temporal extent, and  the rights
(e.g., reproduction, distribution and communication to the public)
expressly]{.stl_10}

[228 ]{.stl_07}[Article 9.2 of the Spanish
]{.stl_09}[Copyright  Act]{.stl_17 style="font-style:italic;"}[:
]{.stl_09}[―]{.stl_08}[A work that constitutes an autonomous creation,
even if published  in]{.stl_17 style="font-style:italic;"}

[conjunction with other works, shall be considered an independent
work.]{.stl_17 style="font-style:italic;"}[‖ ]{.stl_18
style="font-style:italic;"}[229 ]{.stl_07}[Article 43 of the Spanish
]{.stl_09}[Copyright Act]{.stl_17
style="font-style:italic;"}[.]{.stl_09}

[page 81]{.stl_10}

[granted. In the event the parties do not include this information in
the agreement, the rights described above will apply.]{.stl_10}

[Nevertheless, the rights regime is different for salaried authors, as
the Spanish ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[establishes that, in the absence of an
agreement in writing between the]{.stl_10}

[author/employee and the employer, ]{.stl_10}[―it shall be presumed that
the exploitation rights have ]{.stl_19 style="font-style:italic;"}[been
granted exclusively and with the scope necessary for the exercise of the
customary activity of the producer at the time of the delivery of the
work made by virtue of the said ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[employment relations.‖]{.stl_19
style="font-style:italic;"}^[230\ ]{.stl_16}^[However, in this sector,
video game studios usually include in their employment agreements a
stipulation by which the authors transfer their rights, without
limitation, to the company; therefore, only on rare occasions when
studios do not include such clauses would the above-mentioned
presumptions apply.]{.stl_10}

[249. ]{.stl_12}[With respect to the computer software included in a
video game, the presumption of  ownership of the rights is different, as
Article 97.4 of the Spanish ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[ establishes that:]{.stl_10}

[―where a salaried worker creates a computer program in the course of
dutie]{.stl_19 style="font-style:italic;"}[s entrusted to him or on
instructions from his employer, the ownership of the corresponding
exploitation rights in the computer program so created, including both
the source program and the object program, shall belong exclusively to
the employer, unless o]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[therwise
agreed.‖ ]{.stl_19 style="font-style:italic;"}[The difference between
software and other types of works is, therefore, the scope of the rights
presumably transferred being limited in the case of artistic works, and
unlimited for software.]{.stl_10}

[250. ]{.stl_12}[Thus, under Spanish law, it is highly recommended to
include in the agreement between  video game studios and authors the
transfer of rights between the latter to the former.  However, most
video games are created at the initiative and under the direction of a
legal  person, so they can be considered collective works; in that case,
and in the absence of an  agreement to the contrary, the rights in the
collective work (not in the individual elements  that are part of it)
will vest exclusively in the producer.]{.stl_10}

[COMPENSATION]{.stl_10}

[251. ]{.stl_12}[The Spanish ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[is especially protective of authors, as it
considers them the  weakest part of the value chain. Following this
premise, regarding compensation Article ]{.stl_10}[ 46 states that
―]{.stl_15}[the transfer granted by the author for a consideration shall
entitle him to a  proportional share in the proceeds of exploitation,
the amount thereof being agreed upon ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[ with the transferee.‖ ]{.stl_19
style="font-style:italic;"}[However, the
Act]{.stl_10}^[231\ ]{.stl_16}^[acknowledges the difficulty in
determining the  amount in certain cases, as it authorizes the payment
of a lump sum to the author:]{.stl_10}

[- ]{.stl_14}[When, on account of the manner of exploitation, there is
great difficulty in  calculating the proceeds, or where their
verification either is impossible or would  incur costs out of
proportion to the eventual rewards; and]{.stl_10}

[- ]{.stl_14}[Where the work, being used with others, does not
constitute an essential]{.stl_10}

[element of the intellectual creation in which it is embodied.]{.stl_10}

[252. ]{.stl_12}[Therefore, any of these could be used by a video game
studio to justify the payment of a  lump sum instead of a royalty, as
either could be perfectly applicable to a video game,  where the
contribution of a single author usually is not an essential element of
the game  or when it is difficult to calculate the royalty.]{.stl_10}

[230 ]{.stl_07}[Article 52 of the Spanish ]{.stl_09}[Copyright
Act]{.stl_17 style="font-style:italic;"}[. ]{.stl_09}[231
]{.stl_07}[Article 46 of the Spanish ]{.stl_09}[Copyright Act]{.stl_17
style="font-style:italic;"}[.]{.stl_09}

[page 82]{.stl_10}

[253. ]{.stl_12}[In conclusion, Spain does not have a specific legal
framework for video games and, unlike  many other countries, scholars
are skeptical about the applicability of the audiovisual  works regime
for this kind of work. In any case, the Spanish ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act
]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[is quite  developed with respect
to the transfer of rights, which can be perfectly applicable
to  video  games.]{.stl_10}

[URUGUAY]{.stl_11}

[CLASSIFICATION]{.stl_10}

[254. ]{.stl_12}[The applicable legislation regarding copyright
protection in Uruguay is contained in one  statutory law, ]{.stl_10}[Law
No. 9.739 of December 17, 1937, on Copyright ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[(last modified by Law  No. 18,046 of October
24, 2006) (hereinafter, the Uruguayan ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[).  Nevertheless, copyright protection is
also provided for in the ]{.stl_10}[Constitution of the
Oriental  Republic of Uruguay]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[, of
1997, in which Article 33 establishes that ―]{.stl_15}[intellectual
work,  copyright, inventors and artists, will be recognized and
protected by law.]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[‖]{.stl_15}

[255. ]{.stl_12}[Although video games are not specifically foreseen in
the Uruguayan ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[,  these  works have been protected as
copyrighted works since the adoption of an open legal  definition
according to Sections 1 and 5 of the Uruguayan ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act
]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[(as amended by  Law 17.616 of
January 10, 2003). This amendment includes a more detailed list of
works  that may be protected under copyright. As mentioned above, video
games are not  included in this list, which includes new works such
as:]{.stl_10}

[a. Audiovisual works, including cinematographic works, produced and
expressed by  any means or process.]{.stl_10}

[b. Computer programs, whether in source or object code; compilations of
data or  other material, in any form, which by reason of the selection
or arrangement of  their contents constitute intellectual
creations.]{.stl_10}^[232]{.stl_16}^

[256. ]{.stl_12}[A specific legal classification within copyright
applicable to video games has  neither been  included in statutory law
nor has the matter been addressed by ]{.stl_10}[Uruguay's courts or by
]{.stl_15}[ legal scholars (with the exception of certain piracy cases).
Nevertheless, and in the  absence of specific legal guidance, business
practice has shown that video games are  actually protected under
copyright, because the National Registry of Copyrights  recognizes such
intellectual works as it is not necessary, upon registration, to
identify the  nature of the work.]{.stl_10}

[Hence, the issue remains unclear as to whether video games should be
regulated as audiovisual works or as software. However, due to the
nature of video games and the particularities of the industry, a strong
argument can be made in favor of these works being regarded as software
under Uruguay law.]{.stl_10}

[One of the arguments for placing video games under software protection
was mentioned during the third regional seminar on Intellectual Property
for Judges and Prosecutors of Uruguay in 2004. During the seminar,
criminal and piracy cases regarding software were studied and video
games were specifically mentioned. A judge of the Criminal First
Instance Court found liability with respect to parties ]{.stl_10}[―who
reproduced software processes (software and video games) without
permission of the authors and/or assignees.‖]{.stl_19
style="font-style:italic;"}^[233]{.stl_16}^

[232 ]{.stl_07}[Section 5 of the Uruguayan ]{.stl_09}[Copyright
Act]{.stl_17 style="font-style:italic;"}[, as amended by Law 17.616 of
January 10, 2003.]{.stl_09}

[233 ]{.stl_07}[The cases listed are for the Legal Court of First
Instance in Criminal Shift Sixth:]{.stl_09}

[\[Footnote continued on next page\]]{.stl_21}

[page 83]{.stl_10}

[THE BUSINESS REALITY IN URUGUAY]{.stl_10}

[257. ]{.stl_12}[In recent years, Uruguay has become a fast-growing
market for video game production,  mainly due to important tax
incentives for the development of software and Free Trade  Zones that
allow companies to provide services such as programming, with no tax
in  Uruguay to third parties outside the country.]{.stl_10}

[The main stakeholders involved in the recent development of the video
game industry are national freelance programmers that sell their content
to third parties outside Uruguay, and small and medium-sized local
companies that produce content at the request of foreign
companies.]{.stl_10}

[The Ceibal Plan, a governmental entity responsible for the ―One Laptop
Per Child Program‖, which provides every student in public education and
some private institutions ]{.stl_15}[with a laptop for educational
purposes, is also heavily involved in the production of educational
video games. This institution frequently organizes contests in which
freelance programmers, small local companies and even students develop
video games for their ]{.stl_10}[use at the ―Ceibalitas‖ (the name given
]{.stl_15}[to the laptops of the Ceibal Plan).]{.stl_10}

[RIGHTS HOLDERS AND STAKEHOLDERS]{.stl_10}

[258. ]{.stl_12}[The Uruguayan ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[does not provide a legal definition of
]{.stl_10}[author]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[,
although  considering the continental tradition of this country and the
concession of profound moral  rights, it is understood that only natural
persons can become authors. Therefore, legal  persons can acquire
exploitation rights from individuals, as well as the exercise of
moral  rights, although they will never be considered ]{.stl_10}[authors
]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[in this jurisdiction. Authors of
a  video game will be those individuals who contribute to the work,
creating original elements  of any nature (literary, scientific or
artistic).]{.stl_10}

[In this regard, other persons can create original works that could be
incorporated into a video game, for example online gamers using the
toolbox provided by the video game studio. The original contributions by
players or users of a video game should be studied on a case-by-case
basis to analyze the level of authorship of the specific contributions
and to determine whether they can be the subject of protection. Either
way, an assignment clause incorporated within the terms and conditions
of the video game is crucial to ensuring the full exploitation rights
for the video game producer.]{.stl_10}

[259. ]{.stl_12}[As noted above, video games in Uruguay are more likely
to be treated as software, and  this analysis therefore focuses on
authorship and the rights holders, as established for  software
creations under the Uruguayan ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[. Nevertheless, and for the sake of  a
possible future consideration of a distributive classification,
authorship and the rights  holders of an audiovisual work are also
briefly analyzed below.]{.stl_10}

[A. Software]{.stl_10}

[Software can be developed in Uruguay by one or several authors;
therefore, the creation may be a collective work, and the rights equally
shared by the authors involved in the development of the
software.]{.stl_10}^[234\ ]{.stl_16}^[Nonetheless, the Uruguayan
]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act ]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[presumes
that the authors of software have assigned their economic]{.stl_10}

[\[Footnote continued from previous page\]]{.stl_21}

[(a) Mariño Gonzalez, Hugo Daniel, Sheet S 48/93; (b) Natalevich, James
Daniel, Tab S 244/94;]{.stl_09}

[(c) Nappa Pozzi, Raul and others,  Sheet S 46/95.]{.stl_09}

[234 ]{.stl_07}[Sections 27 and 28 of the Uruguayan ]{.stl_09}[Copyright
Act]{.stl_17 style="font-style:italic;"}[.]{.stl_09}

[page 84]{.stl_10}

[rights to the producer in an unlimited and exclusive manner, which also
implies authorization to make decisions regarding disclosure and
exercise of moral rights. Moreover, and unless otherwise agreed, authors
may not oppose changes or successive versions of such creations made or
authorized by the producer. ]{.stl_10}^[235]{.stl_16}^

[In Uruguay, the authors of a software creation can be employees of the
producer; in addition, the employment relationship can be public or
private, and the author/employee can be responsible for the entire
software program or just a portion of it. The presumption for employees
is identical to that of authors with respect to the producer; therefore,
unless otherwise agreed, it is presumed that the employee has
]{.stl_10}[authorized ]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[(as opposed
to assigned) the employer or the person commissioning the work to
exercise, in an unlimited and exclusive manner, the economic rights and
the moral rights in the work.]{.stl_10}^[236]{.stl_16}^

[B. Audiovisual works]{.stl_10}

[Uruguayan law establishes that an audiovisual work is a collective
work, and it is therefore presumed, in the absence of proof to the
contrary, that audiovisual works have the following co-authors: the
director, the screenwriter and the composer, if any, and the animator in
the case of animation. It is also presumed that the authors of the
audiovisual work have assigned their economic rights exclusively to the
producer, who furthermore retains ownership of the right to change or
alter the work, as well as being authorized to make decisions concerning
its exploitation. Moreover, the producer of the audiovisual work may
defend the moral rights in the work without prejudice to the rights of
the authors.]{.stl_10}^[237]{.stl_16}^

[The difference between the regulation of software and audiovisual works
is that the Uruguayan ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Law ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[does not include a specific provision
regarding the employment relationship between the producer and the
authors of an audiovisual work. There is thus no presumption regarding
the assignment or authorization of ]{.stl_10}[the employees'
intellectual work]{.stl_15}[.]{.stl_10}

[260. ]{.stl_12}[Bearing in mind the lack of clarity discussed above
regarding the application to video  games of the regulatory provisions
on software or audiovisual works, in Uruguay it is ]{.stl_10}[ strongly
recommended to include a specific assignment of the author's
intellectual ]{.stl_15}[ property rights in the labor contract to avoid
any risks regarding the real transfer of rights  to the
producer.]{.stl_10}

[COMPENSATION]{.stl_10}

[261. ]{.stl_12}[Compensation for creative contributions is determined
on a contractual basis between the  producer and the independent
contractor or employee. Due to the legal provisions  described above, in
the case of software it is understood that compensation is included
in  the salary as long as the creative activity: (i) is directly related
to the scope of  employment; and (ii) is produced by the employee during
the course of work for the  employer. In such a case, contractual
clarification would be unnecessary.]{.stl_10}

[262. ]{.stl_12}[In the case of audiovisual works, there is no legal
provision regarding the creative  activities of employees, and it would
therefore be advisable to include a stipulation in the  work contract to
establish that all creations taking place in the course of work are
deemed  to be compensated by the agreed salary and that the rights shall
be deemed as]{.stl_10}

[235 ]{.stl_07}[Sections 29.7 and 29.8 of the Uruguayan
]{.stl_09}[Copyright Act]{.stl_17 style="font-style:italic;"}[.
]{.stl_09}[236 ]{.stl_07}[Section 29.9 of the Uruguayan
]{.stl_09}[Copyright Act]{.stl_17
style="font-style:italic;"}[.]{.stl_09}

[237 ]{.stl_07}[Sections 29.2 and 29.3 of the Uruguayan
]{.stl_09}[Copyright Act]{.stl_17
style="font-style:italic;"}[.]{.stl_09}

[page 85]{.stl_10}

[automatically transferred to the producer pursuant to Section 29.3 of
the Uruguayan]{.stl_10}

[Copyright Act]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[. This same
consideration applies to independent contractors.]{.stl_10}

[263. ]{.stl_12}[In conclusion, there is currently no specific
legislation in Uruguay applicable to video  games, although business
practice has confirmed the availability of protection via
public  registries as software and computer programs. Recent trends in
video games and the  evolution of their graphical quality may also lead
to their protection as audiovisual works,  making it likely that Uruguay
will move towards a distributive classification.]{.stl_10}

[SWEDEN]{.stl_11}

[CLASSIFICATION OF VIDEO GAMES]{.stl_10}

[264. ]{.stl_12}[Sweden protects works of authorship through the
―]{.stl_15}[Lag (1960:729) om upphovsrätt till ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[ litterära och konstnärliga verk‖ ]{.stl_19
style="font-style:italic;"}[(]{.stl_10}[Act on Copyright in Literary and
Artistic Works ]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[--
]{.stl_15}[ hereinafter, the Swedish ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[) of 1960, which has been amended
numerous  times.]{.stl_10}^[238\ ]{.stl_16}^[In this regard, Article 1
of the Swedish ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[establishes that:]{.stl_10}

[―]{.stl_15}[anyone who has created a literary or artistic work shall
have copyright in that work, regardless of whether it is 1. a fictional
or descriptive representation in writing or speech; 2. a computer
program; 3. a musical or dramatic work; 4. a cinematographic work; 5. a
photographic work or another work of fine arts; 6. a work of
architecture or applied art; or 7. a work expressed in some other
manner]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[.‖]{.stl_15}

[Although Article 1 of the Swedish ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act ]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[does not expressly mention video games, such
works can definitely be protected within several of the categories of
works described above, or under the general clause of point 7.]{.stl_10}

[Accordingly, video games or computer games can be afforded copyright
protection under Swedish law in two ways. First, the code constituting
the underlying computer program can be protected as a literary work, its
categorical definition being a computer program in accordance with
Section 1(1) paragraph 2 of the Swedish ]{.stl_10}[Copyright
Act]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[. Second, the graphic user
interface or parts of it produced by the computer program (i.e., the
visual output of the screen images of the video game) can be protected,
for instance, as a cinematographic work if it meets the required level
of originality. The issue of whether a graphic user interface can be
protected by copyright is, however, ambiguous and Swedish case law on
the subject is sparse.]{.stl_10}

[265. ]{.stl_12}[In Case C 393-]{.stl_10}[09 the EU Court of Justice
(the ―EUJ‖) ruled that a graphic user interface is ]{.stl_15}[ not a
form of expression of a computer program within the meaning of the
]{.stl_10}[Computer  Program Directive]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}^[239\ ]{.stl_16}^[and therefore cannot be
protected by copyright as a computer  program under that
directive.]{.stl_10}^[240\ ]{.stl_16}^[Nevertheless, such an interface
can be protected under  the ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Directive]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}^[241\ ]{.stl_16}^[if it is the author's own
intellectual creation]{.stl_15}[.]{.stl_10}

[RIGHTS HOLDERS AND STAKEHOLDERS]{.stl_10}

[268. ]{.stl_12}[Considering the complexity of this kind of work, the
number of rights holders and  stakeholders will vary depending on the
video game in question. The number of persons  involved in the creation,
development and marketing of a video game made, for example,  for a
mobile platform such as Android or iOS, will be far fewer than in the
case of a video  game for a console, like PlayStation 3 or Xbox 360. As
a consequence, in general, the  main stakeholders involved in the value
chain and in the chain of rights for video games  on the Swedish market
are the following:]{.stl_10}^[245]{.stl_16}^

[a. Hardware manufacturers (such as console manufacturers and gaming
platform]{.stl_10}

[owners);]{.stl_10}

[b. Game development studios (including in-house and subcontracted
suppliers of]{.stl_10}

[ graphics, sounds and computer code); c. Publishers;]{.stl_10}

[d. Distributors/retailers; and e. Consumers.]{.stl_10}

[269. ]{.stl_12}[Only those persons in the value chain, who create a
literary or artistic work according to  the terms of Section 1 of the
Swedish ]{.stl_10}[Copyright Act]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[,
can be qualified as an ]{.stl_10}[author]{.stl_05
style="font-style:italic;"}[. Under  the ]{.stl_10}[Swedish Copyright
Act]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[, the author is any physical
person who has created a literary or  artistic
work.]{.stl_10}^[246\ ]{.stl_16}^[Thus, a video game production may
include copyright-protected works by, ]{.stl_10}[ inter alia]{.stl_05

designers, sound designers,]{.stl_10}

[composers, motion capture actors, voice actors and game engine
programmers.]{.stl_10}^[247]{.stl_16}^

[270. ]{.stl_12}[Under Swedish copyright law, the rules concerning
initial ownership of a copyrighted work  are based upon the general

Act]{.stl_05 style="font-style:italic;"}[, the protection of
literary  and artistic works includes products of all intellectual
creativity, regardless of the  way in  which they are
expressed.]{.stl_10}^[250\ ]{.stl_16}^[The affirmative scope of

considered a work, it must be the result of an author's own personal
]{.stl_15}[intellectual  creativity.]{.stl_10}

[With regard to the aforesaid, it could be stated that user-generated
